1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""

Now I have a thorough understanding of the problem. Let me analyze the architecture and figure out the best strategy. ## Architecture Analysis The model is one repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B with pattern `("K", "K", "K", "M")` — 3 KDA layers + 1 MLA layer, each followed by a 64-expert MoE FFN. ### Key dimensions: - hidden = 2304 - KDA: 32 heads × 128 dim = 4096 - MLA: 32 heads, kv_lora=512, qk_nope=128, qk_rope=64, v_head=128 - MoE: 64 experts, top-8 routed + 1 shared, inter=1024 - group = 128 ### The decode path per token: Each of 4 blocks does: attention (KDA or MLA) + MoE FFN. ### W4A16 quantization: - weights stored as int4 packed two-per-byte along `in` (w_q, uint8, shape (in//2, out)) - per-group asymmetric dequant params group-size 128 along `in` (scales and zeros, bf16, shape (in//128, out)) - dequant: w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n] ### The key bottleneck: At batch=1, decode is bandwidth-bound on weight traffic. The int4 weights are 1/4 the bytes of bf16. Reference and baseline both materialize the full bf16 weight then matmul — that throws away the bandwidth advantage. The job is to fuse int4 unpack + per-group dequant directly into the GEMV so we stream int4 weights once. ### Scoring: - `score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`, geomean over context lengths 2048, 8192, 16384 - Correctness: cosine similarity >= 0.98 for next-token hidden, KDA state, MLA cache ### Let me think about what dominates decode latency: Per block: 1. **KDA layers (3 of them)**: - q_proj, k_proj, v_proj, g_proj: each (2304 → 4096), 4 linears - o_proj: (4096 → 2304) - State recurrence ops (small) - Total: 5 quant linears per KDA block 2. **MLA layer (1)**: - q_proj: (2304 → 32*(128+64)=6144) - kv_a: (2304 → 512+64=576) - kv_b: (512 → 32*(128+128)=8192) — but applied to full cache [ctx+1, 512] → this is a GEMM not GEMV! [ctx+1, 512] @ [512, 8192] - o_proj: (32*128=4096 → 2304) - Attention over the cache 3. **MoE FFN (4 of them)**: - router: bf16 (2304 → 64), tiny - gate, up: each (2304 → 1024), 64 experts, top-8 used - down: each (1024 → 2304), 64 experts, top-8 used - shared: gate_s, up_s (2304 → 1024), down_s (1024 → 2304), 1 expert - So per MoE: 8 routed experts × 3 + 1 shared × 3 = 27 expert matmuls of size (2304→1024) or (1024→2304) ### Memory traffic analysis (the bottleneck): The big weight matrices that dominate traffic: - KDA block: q/k/v/g_proj each (2304×4096), o_proj (4096×2304). Per block: 4×2304×4096 + 4096×2304 = 5×2304×4096 int4... actually let me compute in int4 bytes. - q_proj: 2304*4096 / 2 = 4.7M int4 bytes - Same for k,v,g: 4.7M each → 4 × 4.7M = 18.9M - o_proj: 4096*2304/2 = 4.7M - Total per KDA block weights (int4): ~23.6 MB. Plus scales/zeros: small (ng = 2304/128=18 groups, × 4096 × 2 bytes × 2 (s+z) = small). - MoE block: For batch=1, only 8 routed experts + 1 shared expert are used per FFN. Each expert: - gate: 2304*1024/2 = 1.18M - up: 2304*1024/2 = 1.18M - down: 1024*2304/2 = 1.18M - Total per expert: 3.54M int4 bytes - 9 experts used: 31.8M int4 bytes per MoE block So per full motif (3 KDA + 1 MLA + 4 MoE): - KDA: 3 × 23.6 = 70.8 MB - MLA: q_proj 2304*6144/2=7.08M, kv_a 2304*576/2=0.66M, kv_b 512*8192/2=2.1M, o_proj 4096*2304/2=4.7M → ~14.5 MB - MoE: 4 × 31.8 = 127.2 MB Total int4 weight traffic ≈ 70.8 + 14.5 + 127.2 ≈ 212 MB per token. At 1.8 TB/s, that's 212e-3 / 1.8e3 = 0.118 ms per token just for weight streaming in the ideal case. So the theoretical floor is ~0.12 ms/tok, giving ~8500 tok/s. Wait, but the MoE traffic depends on whether we read all 64 experts or just the active ones. At batch=1 we only need 8+1=9 experts, so we only stream those 9 experts. Good. The baseline reads all needed experts too (it does `_batch_wbf(self.gate, idx)` selecting only the top-8). Actually wait — baseline selects idx for gate/up/down and sidx for shared. So baseline also only reads 9 experts. Good. But baseline materializes them as bf16 first (9 experts × 3 matrices × 1024 × 2304 × 2 bytes = ... actually it's the dequant write then bf16 read). So baseline reads int4 + writes bf16 + reads bf16 = roughly 9 experts × 3 × (1.18M read int4 + 2.36M write bf16 + 2.36M read bf16) — the bf16 materialization dominates. Actually, let me reconsider. The fused kernel streams int4 weights ONCE. So: - Fused: 212 MB / 1.8 TB/s = 0.118 ms/tok ideal - Baseline (materializes): reads int4 (212MB... wait no). Hmm, actually for the big projection linears (q_proj etc.), baseline does `x @ _wbf(self.q_proj)` which dequantizes to bf16 [2304, 4096] = 18.9 MB write + 18.9 MB read, on top of 4.7 MB int4 read. So ~42 MB per q_proj vs 4.7 MB fused. That's a 9x traffic difference for those. So the fused approach should give a big speedup, primarily from: 1. Not materializing bf16 weights for the big projections (q/k/v/g/o_proj in KDA and MLA) 2. Not materializing bf16 weights for MoE experts ### Strategy: Triton fused int4 dequant-GEMV kernel I'll write a Triton kernel that does: - Input: x [M, K] (M=1 for GEMV mostly, except kv_b which is [ctx, 512]) - int4 weight w_q [K//2, N] - scales [K//128, N], zeros [K//128, N] - Output: y [M, N] = x @ dequant(w) For M=1, this is a GEMV (matrix-vector). For kv_b it's a GEMM with M=ctx (up to 16384). Wait, the kv_b is the expensive MLA op at long context. Let me think: kv_b is (512 → 8192), applied to [ctx, 512] cache. So that's [ctx, 512] @ [512, 8192] = [ctx, 8192]. At ctx=16384, that's a GEMM of M=16384, K=512, N=8192. The weight kv_b is only 512×8192 int4 = 2.1 MB, but the cache is ctx×512 bf16 = 16384×512×2 = 16.8 MB. So at long context, the kv_b matmul reads the cache (16.8 MB) + weight (2.1 MB). This is more of a compute/attention-bound thing at long context. Actually wait — but there's the MLA "absorb" optimization. In the reference, `kvb = st["c_kv"] @ _wbf(self.kv_b)` produces [ctx, H, qk_nope+v_head] = [ctx, 32, 256]. Then attention is computed. The absorb trick: instead of projecting the full cache through kv_b and then attending, you can project the query through (kv_b)^T absorb... Let me think. The MLA computation: - q_nope [H, qk_nope=128], q_rope [H, qk_rope=64] - kv_a(x) → c_kv [512], k_rope [64] - cache c_kv [ctx, 512] - kvb = c_kv_cache @ W_kv_b → [ctx, H, 256], split into k_nope [ctx,H,128], v [ctx,H,128] - scores = einsum("hd,lhd->lh", q_nope, k_nope) + einsum("hd,ld->lh", q_rope, k_rope) - o = einsum("lh,lhd->hd", p, v) The absorb optimization for MLA: - k_nope = c_kv @ W_kvb_nope where W_kvb_nope is [512, H, 128] - The score from q_nope: sum_h q_nope[h] · k_nope[l,h] = sum_h sum_{r=512} q_nope[h,d] c_kv[l,r] W_kvb_nope[r,h,d] = sum_r c_kv[l,r] (sum_h sum_d q_nope[h,d] W_kvb_nope[r,h,d]) = c_kv · q_absorbed where q_absorbed[r] = sum_{h,d} q_nope[h,d] W_kvb_nope[r,h,d] So we can absorb kv_b into the query: q'_nope = q_nope @ W_kvb_nope^T → [512], then score_nope = c_kv @ q'_nope [ctx]. This avoids materializing [ctx, H, 128] k_nope! That's huge at long context. But the value path: v = c_kv @ W_kvb_v, and o = sum_l p[l] v[l]. We still need v for the value aggregation, OR we can absorb differently: o[h,d] = sum_l p[l] v[l,h,d] = sum_l p[l] sum_r c_kv[l,r] W_kvb_v[r,h,d] = sum_r (sum_l p[l] c_kv[l,r]) W_kvb_v[r,h,d] = (p @ c_kv) @ W_kvb_v where p@ c_kv is [512] (weighted sum of latent cache). Then o = [512] @ W_kvb_v → [H, 128]. So: absorb both! - q_absorbed = q_nope @ W_kvb_nope^T [512] (small) - scores_nope = c_kv_cache @ q_absorbed [ctx] (cheap: [ctx,512] @ [512]) - p = softmax(scores_nope + scores_rope) [ctx] - latent_v = p @ c_kv_cache [512] (cheap: [ctx]·[ctx,512]) - o = latent_v @ W_kvb_v [H, 128] (small: [512]→[H*128]) This means we NEVER materialize [ctx, H, 256]. We only do two [ctx,512] dot-products and small linears. This is the FlashMLA / DeepSeek MLA absorb trick. This is the key MLA optimization that makes long context cheap. The reference and baseline both do the materialize path (kvb = c_kv @ kv_b → [ctx, H, 256]) which is O(ctx × H × 256) memory and compute. The absorb path is O(ctx × 512) which is 2x cheaper in K-dim but more importantly avoids materializing the big intermediate. Actually wait — both c_kv (512) and the materialized kvb (256 per head × 32 heads = 8192) — kvb has more columns. c_kv @ kv_b is [ctx, 512]@[512,8192]. The absorbed path does c_kv @ q_absorbed [ctx,512]@[512,1] = ctx×512 work, plus p@c_kv [1,ctx]@[ctx,512]=512, plus latent_v @ W_kvb_v [1,512]@[512,8192]=512×8192. So absorbed is dramatically cheaper: ctx×512 + 512 + 512×8192 vs ctx×8192×(read+write) + ctx×256×H attention. So the MLA absorb is critical for long context. This is mentioned in the prompt: "the absorb-vs-materialize fork in MLA decides the whole thing". OK so my strategy: 1. **Fused int4 dequant-GEMV Triton kernel** for all the QuantLinear ops (batch=1 GEMV) and a version that handles the small GEMMs. 2. **MLA absorb** to avoid materializing the long-context kv_b output. 3. **CUDA graphs** to cut launch overhead (many small kernel launches at batch=1). 4. Possibly **fp16/bf16 accumulation** tuning. Let me think about whether to use Triton or CUDA. Triton is allowed (the framework.txt detection shows triton is a recognized category). Let me use Triton — it's the most practical. Actually, let me reconsider. The problem says "you must write the fused int4 dequant-GEMV yourself - do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel". Triton is fine. CUDA C++ via load_inline is also fine ("cuda_raw" is a recognized category). Let me think about which gives the best perf. For batch-1 GEMV on Blackwell, a well-tuned Triton kernel can get close to peak bandwidth. Let me go with Triton for portability and iteration speed, but I should consider the structure carefully. ### Triton kernel design for int4 dequant-GEMV The classic approach: each thread block computes a tile of the output [BLOCK_N]. For each output column n in [n_start, n_start+BLOCK_N): - accumulator = 0 - for each group g along K (each group is 128 elements): - load scale[g, n], zero[g, n] - for k in group: load int4 weight w[k,n], unpack, load x[k], accumulate (w - zero)*scale * x[k] But to be efficient, we parallelize across N (output) and vectorize across K. A common Triton GEMV kernel: - Grid: (N // BLOCK_N,) — or 2D with split-K - Each program handles BLOCK_N output columns, loops over K in tiles of BLOCK_K - Load x tile [BLOCK_K], load weight tile [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] (from int4), dequant on the fly, accumulate For batch-1, M=1, so it's a matrix-vector. The weight tile load + dequant is the bandwidth-limited part. Let me design: - BLOCK_N = 64 or 128 (output columns per program) - BLOCK_K = 128 (= group size, natural for dequant) — load a full group at once - Load x[BLOCK_K] once (shared across all N in the block) — actually x is read repeatedly, but it's small (2304 elements = 4.6KB bf16), fits in L1/shared. Actually for GEMV, the efficient layout: each program computes BLOCK_N outputs. It loops over K in chunks of BLOCK_K. For each chunk: - Load x_chunk [BLOCK_K] (bf16) — broadcast - Load w_q chunk [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] (int4) → unpack to [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] - Load scales/zeros [BLOCK_K//128, BLOCK_N] - Dequant and FMA into acc[BLOCK_N] The weight load dominates. BLOCK_K=128 means per program per K-iteration we load BLOCK_K//2 × BLOCK_N bytes = 64 × BLOCK_N bytes of int4. Plus 1 scale + 1 zero per column (the group). Let me think about the int4 unpack in Triton. The packed format: w_q[k//2, n] holds w[2k, n] in low nibble and w[2k+1, n] in high nibble. So for even rows, w = w_q & 0xF; for odd rows, w = w_q >> 4. In Triton, I load w_q as uint8 [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N], then: ```python w_lo = (w_q & 0xF).to(bf16) # even K w_hi = (w_q >> 4).to(bf16) # odd K ``` But these are interleaved in K. Hmm. To get w[k] aligned with x[k], I need to handle the interleaving. Option A: Process pairs of K elements. Load w_q[BLOCK_K//2], and x pairs. But the accumulation wants w[k]*x[k]. Let me reconsider. Let me define the kernel over K tiles of BLOCK_K = 128 (= 1 group). Within a tile: - Load x[128] bf16. - Load w_q[64, BLOCK_N] uint8. - Unpack: w_even[i,n] = (w_q[i,n] & 0xF), w_odd[i,n] = (w_q[i,n] >> 4), where w_even corresponds to K=2i, w_odd to K=2i+1. - We want acc += sum_{i} (w_even[i,n] * x[2i] + w_odd[i,n] * x[2i+1]) ... but with dequant (subtract zero, multiply scale). - scale is [1, BLOCK_N] (one per group since group=128=BLOCK_K), zero [1, BLOCK_N]. To accumulate: acc[n] += scale[n] * ( sum_i (w_even[i,n] - zero[n]) * x[2i] + (w_odd[i,n] - zero[n]) * x[2i+1] ) Hmm, but I can factor: (w - zero) * scale * x. Since zero and scale are per-group (constant over the 128 K), I can pull scale out but zero interacts with x. = scale[n] * ( sum_i w_even[i,n]*x[2i] + w_odd[i,n]*x[2i+1] - zero[n]*sum_i(x[2i]+x[2i+1]) ) = scale[n] * ( sum_i w_even[i,n]*x[2i] + w_odd[i,n]*x[2i+1] - zero[n]*sum_k x[k] ) So I can compute sum_k x[k] once per group (a scalar per program), and the weighted sum sum_i w_even*x_even + w_odd*x_odd, then combine. Actually simpler: just dequant inline. Triton handles bf16 fine. Let me just do: ``` w = unpack to bf16 [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] (conceptually) acc += sum_k (w[k,n] - zero[n]) * scale[n] * x[k] ``` But Triton wants me to express the unpacked weight. Let me think about how to load x aligned with unpacked w. Alternative: I store/process x in a way that matches. Actually, the cleanest in Triton: load x[2i] and x[2i+1] separately, or interleave. Hmm, let me reconsider with a cleaner tile. Let me set BLOCK_K to be the number of packed rows × 2. Actually let me just load w_q as [PACKED_K, BLOCK_N] where PACKED_K = BLOCK_K // 2, and x_packed_even [PACKED_K], x_packed_odd [PACKED_K] derived from x. Then: ```python # k_offsets for even: 2*i; odd: 2*i+1 x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + 2*arange) # [PACKED_K] x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + 2*arange + 1) wq = tl.load(wq_ptr + ...) # [PACKED_K, BLOCK_N] uint8 w_even = (wq & 0xF).to(tl.bfloat16) # [PACKED_K, BLOCK_N] w_odd = (wq >> 4).to(tl.bfloat16) # zero, scale: [BLOCK_N] for this group acc += scale * ( (w_even - zero) * x_even[:,None] + (w_odd - zero) * x_odd[:,None] ) summed over PACKED_K ``` This works. The dequant subtracts zero (broadcast over K) and multiplies scale. x_even and x_odd are [PACKED_K] broadcast over N. Wait, `(w_even - zero) * x_even[:,None]`: w_even is [PACKED_K, BLOCK_N], zero is [BLOCK_N] (broadcast), x_even[:,None] is [PACKED_K, 1]. Result [PACKED_K, BLOCK_N], sum over axis 0 → [BLOCK_N]. Good. This is clean. Let me go with this. Now, for performance, the key is: 1. Memory coalescing of the int4 load. w_q layout is [K//2, N] row-major (contiguous in N). So loading a [PACKED_K, BLOCK_N] tile with consecutive N is coalesced. Good. 2. x is small, loaded repeatedly — keep it in registers/shared. For a GEMV with M=1, the arithmetic intensity is low (1 mul-add per 0.5 byte of weight read + 2 bytes... wait, int4 is 0.5 bytes per weight element). For each output element we read K weight elements = K/2 bytes int4 + K/128 × 4 bytes (scale+zero bf16 = 2×2) ≈ K/2 bytes. So 0.5 bytes/element arithmetic. At bf16 throughput this is purely bandwidth bound. Good — fused kernel should hit bandwidth. The bandwidth lower bound: for the q_proj (K=2304, N=4096): int4 = 2304×4096/2 = 4.72 MB, scales+zeros = (2304/128)×4096×4 = 18×4096×4 = 0.29 MB. Total ~5 MB. Plus x read (small). At 1.8 TB/s → 5e6/1.8e12 = 2.8 µs. So q_proj fused ≈ 2.8 µs vs baseline materializing ~42MB → 23 µs. That's the win. ### MoE experts For MoE, each expert is a separate small weight. Key architecture insights:

  • 4 blocks (3 KDA + 1 MLA), each with a 64-expert MoE FFN (top-8 + 1 shared).
  • Bandwidth-bound at batch-1: ~212MB of int4 weights/token streamed. Reference/baseline materialize bf16 weights (~9× traffic). The win is a fused int4 dequant-GEMV.
  • MLA "absorb" trick is critical for long context: instead of c_kv @ kv_b → [L, H, 256] (materializes 268MB at L=16384), absorb kv_b into the query/value so cache ops stay [L, 512]. This is the "absorb-vs-materialize fork".
  • Launch overhead will dominate (~210 kernels/step) unless I use CUDA graphs.

=== rest of gpu-lock-exec ===
if [ -f "$owner_file" ]; then
    IFS=$'\t' read -r owner_pid owner_run_dir < "$owner_file" || true
    if [ "${owner_run_dir:-}" = "${RUN_DIR:-}" ] && kill -0 "${owner_pid:-}" 2>/dev/null; then

fi
{

Let me write it. ```python import triton import triton.language as tl @triton.jit def _w4_gemv_kernel( X, WQ, S, Z, Y, K, N, stride_xk, stride_wqk, stride_wqn, stride_sk, stride_sn, GROUP: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, SPLIT_K: tl.constexpr, ): pid_n = tl.program_id(0) pid_k = tl.program_id(1) offs_n = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = offs_n < N # K range for this split k_tiles_total = K // GROUP tiles_per_split = (k_tiles_total + SPLIT_K - 1) // SPLIT_K g_start = pid_k * tiles_per_split g_end = tl.minimum((pid_k + 1) * tiles_per_split, k_tiles_total) acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) HALF: tl.constexpr = GROUP // 2 pk = tl.arange(0, HALF) for g in range(g_start, g_end): k_base = g * GROUP s = tl.load(S + g * stride_sk + offs_n * stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) z = tl.load(Z + g * stride_sk + offs_n * stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) x_even = tl.load(X + (k_base + 2 * pk) * stride_xk).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(X + (k_base + 2 * pk + 1) * stride_xk).to(tl.float32) wq_off = (k_base // 2 + pk)[:, None] * stride_wqk + offs_n[None, :] * stride_wqn wq_t = tl.load(WQ + wq_off, mask=n_mask[None, :], other=0) we = (wq_t & 0xF).to(tl.float32) wo = (wq_t >> 4).to(tl.float32) contrib = (we - z[None, :]) * x_even[:, None] + (wo - z[None, :]) * x_odd[:, None] acc += tl.sum(contrib, axis=0) * s if SPLIT_K == 1: tl.store(Y + offs_n, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) else: tl.store(Y + pid_k * N + offs_n, acc) # partials fp32 ``` Hmm wait, the partials write: Y is [SPLIT_K, N] when SPLIT_K>1. +"""Fast W4A16 decode for one Kimi-Linear hybrid motif (batch=1).
+
+Beats baseline.py by (1) fusing int4 unpack + per-group dequant directly into the
+GEMV so the int4 weights are streamed once (never materialized as bf16), and
+(2) the MLA *absorb* trick: instead of projecting the whole growing latent cache
+through kv_b (which would materialize an [L, H, 256] tensor -- ~268MB at L=16384),
+absorb kv_b into the query/value so every cache op stays O(L * kv_lora).
+
+Module tree / buffer names are identical to the reference so the reference
+state_dict loads with strict=True. All int4 dequant-GEMV kernels below are
+hand-written Triton (no prebuilt quant library).
+"""
+from __future__ import annotations

The model is W4A16: the big projection weights are stored as 4-bit integers, the activations and accumulation are bf16. reference.py is the correctness oracle and baseline.py is an already-optimized pure-PyTorch version; your job is to go beyond baseline.py. Your answer goes in solution.py and must expose the same Model class and the same step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state) interface as reference.py, with identical buffer/parameter names so it loads the reference weights. Make whatever mess you want in this directory otherwise.  The unit is one repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B: four layers in the pattern [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA], each followed by a 64-expert MoE FFN (top-8 routed + 1 shared, SiLU, intermediate 1024), with RMSNorm and residuals. Hidden size is 2304. The KDA layers are gated-delta linear attention with 32 heads of dim 128, a short causal depthwise conv (kernel 4) on q/k/v, a per-channel log-decay gate, and a per-head write strength; they carry a fixed-size recurrent state S[32,128,128] plus the conv window across decode steps. The MLA layers are multi-head latent attention with 32 heads, a kv_lora_rank of 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128, decoupled RoPE (theta 10000); they carry a growing compressed latent KV cache (the 512-d latent plus the 64-d rope key per past token).  The weights are quantized W4A16, AWQ/GPTQ-style: per linear, the (in, out) weight is stored as int4 packed two-per-byte along `in` (w_q, uint8, shape (in//2, out)), with per-group asymmetric dequant params group-size 128 along `in` (scales and zeros, bf16, shape (in//128, out)). Dequant is w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n]. The 64 MoE experts (and the shared expert) are stored the same way, one int4 weight set per expert. reference.py has the exact pack/unpack/dequant code, the layer math, the state layout, and how step consumes one token and updates both kinds of state. Read it.  At batch 1 this decode is bandwidth-bound on the weight stream, and the int4 weights are a quarter of the bytes of bf16 - but only if you never materialize the dequantized bf16 weight. reference.py and baseline.py both unpack each weight to a full bf16 matrix and then matmul, which throws the bandwidth advantage away (int4 read plus bf16 write plus bf16 read). The job is to fuse the int4 unpack and per-group dequant directly into the GEMV so you stream the int4 weights once. You are decoding one new token against a context that is already `context_len` tokens long (the states and caches are pre-filled). Correctness is the next-token hidden vector and the updated decode state matching reference.py by cosine similarity (>= 0.98), checked from an identical fed state across several seeds and context lengths. You are scored on decode latency relative to baseline.py, geomean over context lengths 2048, 8192, and 16384.  This needs to be real. Plain bf16 matmul/linear is allowed, but you must write the fused int4 dequant-GEMV yourself - do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel (bitsandbytes, torchao, marlin, gptq/awq, exllama) or a prebuilt model/attention/MoE stack (transformers, fla, flash_mla, vllm, sglang), or reference.py / baseline.py. If you need background, look up W4A16 / AWQ / GPTQ dequant-GEMV kernels, the Kimi-Linear architecture, KDA / gated DeltaNet, multi-head latent attention, and Blackwell/Triton decode kernels yourself. Let me think about the grouped kernel carefully. Grouped W4A16 GEMV kernel: - Inputs: - X: pointer to activations. For shared-x: x [K], stride_xe=0. For per-expert: X [E, K], stride_xe=K. - WQ: [E, K//2, N] uint8. strides (we, wqk, wqn) = (K//2*N, N, 1). - S, Z: [E, ng, N] bf16. strides (se, sg, sn) = (ng*N, N, 1). - Y: [E, N] bf16 (or [E, SPLIT_K, N] partials). - Grid: (E, n_blocks, SPLIT_K). - Each program: pid_e, pid_n, pid_k. Same as single GEMV but with expert offset. Let me write it. The body is the single GEMV body with base offsets pid_e*stride_we etc. For the output store: Y[pid_e, pid_k, offs_n] for partial, Y[pid_e, offs_n] for direct. Let me write the unified kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _w4_gemv_grp_kernel( X, WQ, S, Z, Y, K, N, stride_xe, stride_xk, stride_we, stride_wqk, stride_wqn, stride_se, stride_sg, stride_sn, stride_ye, GROUP: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, SPLIT_K: tl.constexpr, IS_PARTIAL: tl.constexpr, ): pid_e = tl.program_id(0) pid_n = tl.program_id(1) pid_k = tl.program_id(2) offs_n = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = offs_n < N x_base = X + pid_e * stride_xe wq_base = WQ + pid_e * stride_we s_base = S + pid_e * stride_se z_base = Z + pid_e * stride_se ng = K // GROUP tiles_per_split = (ng + SPLIT_K - 1) // SPLIT_K g_start = pid_k * tiles_per_split g_end = tl.minimum((pid_k+1)*tiles_per_split, ng) acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) pk = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) for g in range(g_start, g_end): s = tl.load(s_base + g*stride_sg + offs_n*stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) z = tl.load(z_base + g*stride_sg + offs_n*stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) acc_g = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) gbase = g * GROUP for ck in tl.static_range(0, GROUP, BLOCK_K): kbase = gbase + ck x_even = tl.load(x_base + (kbase + 2*pk)*stride_xk).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_base + (kbase + 2*pk + 1)*stride_xk).to(tl.float32) w_off = wq_base + (kbase//2 + pk)[:,None]*stride_wqk + offs_n[None,:]*stride_wqn wq = tl.load(w_off, mask=n_mask[None,:], other=0) we = (wq & 0xF).to(tl.float32); wo = (wq >> 4).to(tl.float32) contrib = (we - z[None,:])*x_even[:,None] + (wo - z[None,:])*x_odd[:,None] acc_g += tl.sum(contrib, axis=0) acc += acc_g * s y_base = Y + pid_e * stride_ye if IS_PARTIAL: tl.store(y_base + pid_k*N + offs_n, acc, mask=n_mask) else: tl.store(y_base + offs_n, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Wrapper: ```python def w4_gemv(x, wq, scales, zeros, group): # single (E=1) # x [K], wq [K//2,N], scales/zeros [ng,N] -> [N] K = x.shape[0]; N = wq.shape[1] out = _launch_grouped(x, wq.unsqueeze(0), scales.unsqueeze(0), zeros.unsqueeze(0), K, N, 1, group, stride_xe=0, shared=True) return out[0] # [N] ``` Hmm, but unsqueeze creates views — fine. Actually for E=1 the grouped kernel with grid (1, n_blocks, split_k) works. +import os, sys, time
+import torch
+import reference, solution, shapes
+
+def run(model, cfg, ctx, steps, seed):
+    h = reference.init_token(cfg, seed)
+    st = reference.init_state(cfg, ctx, seed)
+    with torch.no_grad():
+        for _ in range(steps):
+            h, st = model.step(h, st)
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    return h
+
